        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2041d4dd1baf4d7b98cc585d4780e267">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '03dd6a05-51af-4fab-a59c-5c06464d6847'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                var session_id = '14988B70A7FAADDD7BCF77B1DF92A8EF';

                s.src = url;

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d1015262" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Porous carbon has been widely focused to solve the problems of low coulombic efficiency (ICE) and low multiplication capacity of Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) anodes. The superior energy storage properties of two-dimensional(2D)&nbsp;carbon nanosheets can be realized by modulating the structure, but be limited by the carbon sources, making it challenging to obtain 2D structures with large surface area. In this work, a new method for forming carbon materials with high N/S doping content based on combustion activation using the dual activation effect of K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>/KNO<sub>3</sub> is proposed. The synthesized carbon material as an anode for SIBs has a high reversible capacity of 344.44&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at the current density of 5&nbsp;Ag<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity remained at 143.08&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. And the ICE of sodium-ion in ether electrolytes is ≈2.5 times higher than that in ester electrolytes. The sodium storage mechanism of ether/ester-based electrolytes is further explored through ex-situ characterizations. The disparity in electrochemical performance can be ascribed to the discrepancy in kinetics, wherein ether-based electrolytes exhibit a higher rate of Na<sup>+</sup> storage and shedding compared to ester-based electrolytes. This work suggests an effective way to develop doubly doped carbon anode materials for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202308684-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202308684-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the rapid development of the economy, the overuse of fossil fuels is the main cause of energy depletion and environmental pollution. The development of high-performance/low-cost materials for large-scale electrochemical energy storage batteries is imperative.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0001_R_d1015254e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#smll202308684-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0003_R_d1015254e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Chem. A">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have become a research hotspot for large-scale energy storage systems due to abundant sodium reserves, similar electrochemical behavior to Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), and reduced production costs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0004_R_d1015254e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a>-<a href="#smll202308684-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0006_R_d1015254e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Graphite serves as the current commercial anode material for LIBs, but cannot be used as a sodium storage anode material for SIBs due to the weak interactions between sodium ions and graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0007_R_d1015254e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon is a very promising candidate material for SIBs due to its high reversible capacity and low voltage plateau.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0008_R_d1015254e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202308684-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0009_R_d1015254e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, due to the large radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02&nbsp;Å), the slow diffusion kinetics during the electrochemical reaction. An effective strategy to improve sodium storage capacity is to adjust the pore structure and morphology of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0010_R_d1015254e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, porous hard carbon anodes suffer from severe volume expansion during charging and discharging, low coulombic efficiency (ICE), low multiplicative capacity, and poor sodium storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0011_R_d1015254e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Int. J. Energy Res.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, there is a need to design a carbon material with fast reaction kinetics, suppressed volume expansion, and excellent sodium storage performance as an anode.</p>
         
         <p>Several works have demonstrated that the energy storage properties of carbon materials can be effectively improved by modulating the structure/morphology of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0012_R_d1015254e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The easy synthesis route and high surface area of porous carbon facilitate electrolyte penetration and reduce the ion diffusion distance, showing superior sodium storage properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0013_R_d1015254e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Diamond Relat. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhang et al<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0014_R_d1015254e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared columnar porous carbon blocks constructed from amine-functionalized carbon nanosheets using acyl-amino-rich modified polyacrylonitrile as a carbon source, resulting in the rapid storage of Na<sup>+</sup> up to 347.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>However, it is difficult to obtaintwo-dimensional(2D) structures with a large specific surface area caused by the limitation of the carbon source.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0015_R_d1015254e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a>, <a href="#smll202308684-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0016_R_d1015254e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Porous carbons obtained by carbonizing polymer or biomass precursors and then activating them have complex and unpredictable porous structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0017_R_d1015254e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Salt templates such as NaCl, KCl, and K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> are cost-effective, stable, and easy to remove by washing with water. It is possible to control the salt crystallization process by adjusting the specific surface area and porosity of the carbon material. Qiu<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0018_R_d1015254e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J Energy Chem">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et al prepared porous hard-soft composite carbon using NaCl as a template and phenolic resin and pitch as carbon precursors. Luo<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0019_R_d1015254e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et al also reported the preparation of N/O-doped 2D PCNS with a very high specific surface area of 1248&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> by using zinc nitrate hexahydrate as the oxidant and urea as the fuel. Among them, 2D nanocarbon sheets due to their short diffusion distance and the openness of the block-exposed surface, the electrolyte can easily approach the sheet structure, which endows the block electrode materials with excellent charging/discharging performance and good cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0020_R_d1015254e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Fuels">20</a>-<a href="#smll202308684-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0022_R_d1015254e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Heteroatom doping was an effective strategy to improve the physical and chemical properties of hard carbon and enhance the capacity of carbon materials in all aspects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0023_R_d1015254e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> S/N co-doping has higher sodium ion storage capacity compared to single-atom doped materials. Nitrogen doping induces defect formation on the carbon surface, while S doping widens the interlayer spacing and acts as an active site.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0024_R_d1015254e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Homogeneous and steady S doping was difficult to achieve with existing processes, and a common way to achieve sulfur doping is by pyrolysis with a solid sulfur source. However, the sulfur doping content of the solid-state sulfur source is low, and the configuration of S in the electrode material is unstable and prone to fracture from the carbon substrate, resulting in poor cycling stability performance of the electrode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0025_R_d1015254e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Alloys Compd.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yang et al prepared N, S co-doped porous carbon nanosheets as SIBs anode by H<sub>2</sub>S/Ar gas mixture. The S─N/C had a high capacity of 110&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, exceeding nitrogen-doped N/C anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0026_R_d1015254e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the gaseous sulfur source used is usually flammable and toxic, which is not conducive to large-scale production. Therefore, it is worth exploring to find an economical and convenient method to prepare heteroatom-doped carbon with a specific structure.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, the N and S co-doped porous carbon (SXL-5) was prepared by a simple and novel route. N-allylthiourea (S) was used as a self-doped precursor containing N and S, and the soluble salt of potassium sulfate (L) was used as a sulfur source and as a salt templating agent to assist the combustion activation reaction of potassium nitrate (X) with the precursor for the preparation of porous carbon flakes containing N/S dopants (SXL-5). As the anode for SIBs, SXL-5 exhibits a high reversible capacity of 344.44&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in ether-based electrolyte. Even at a large current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity can still be maintained at 143.08&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Based on ex-situ characterizations, the excellent performance of SXL-5 was due to the pyridinium nitrogen being sodiated during the discharge process, and the Na<sup>+</sup> is formed through internal disproportionation reactions of the sodium sulfonate and polysulfate complexes, thereby ensuring good stability during cycling of the material. The difference in electrochemical performance can be attributed to the kinetics of the ether–ester electrolyte, with the ether-based electrolyte storing and shedding Na<sup>+</sup> at a higher rate than the ester-based electrolyte. This work is expected to provide a generalized strategy for the synthesis of doubly doped carbon anodes, as well as a new understanding of the energy storage mechanism of ether/ester-based electrolytes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202308684-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202308684-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202308684-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202308684-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis, Structure, and Chemistry</h3>
            
            <p>N/S-doped porous carbon materials were prepared by a novel combustion activation strategy, and the preparation process can be seen in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308684-fig-0001">1a</a>. The concept of combustion activation is attributed to the combustion synthesis reaction method, which produces gaseous products that can undergo further activation reactions with the carbon material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0027_R_d1015254e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Materials Science">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the pyrolysis process, the gas generated from the combustion-activation reaction with the precursor plays the role of pore-making (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202308684-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>), where potassium nitrate acted as the combustion activator and potassium sulfate (L) acted as sulfur source /salt templating agent (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202308684-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>) and a synergistic activator, creating pores and defects on the surface of the carbon material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0028_R_d1015254e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Sci. (Weinh)">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202308684-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/72e49f18-1ee9-47b6-9d2e-c4053fad7aeb/smll202308684-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202308684-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ee31825b-b344-4401-ab51-f9f150505cdd/smll202308684-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202308684:smll202308684-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/smll202308684-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>

                              </mfenced>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>

                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>

                              <mi>CO</mi>

                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi>

                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{CO}}_2\left( {\mathrm{g}} \right) + {\mathrm{C}}\left( {\mathrm{s}} \right) \to 2{\mathrm{CO}}\left( {\mathrm{g}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202308684-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b0f380b4-3bf8-4571-908c-abf133792a16/smll202308684-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202308684:smll202308684-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/smll202308684-math-0004.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi>CO</mi>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{\mathrm{H}}}_2{\mathrm{O}}\left( {\mathrm{g}} \right) + {\mathrm{C}}\left( {\mathrm{s}} \right) \to {{\mathrm{H}}}_2\left( {\mathrm{g}} \right) + {\mathrm{CO}}\left( {\mathrm{g}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>To investigate the effect of different activation methods and salt templates on the material properties, samples of S-5, SX-5, and SL were prepared for comparison. Measurements using in situ thermogravimetric analysis infrared spectroscopy (TG-IR) are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0001">1b,c</a>. Further increasing the temperature to 200&nbsp;°C, the mass of the sample started to decrease significantly, which was caused by the combustion activation of N-allylthiourea and potassium nitrate, as evidenced by the high absorption peaks of SO<sub>2</sub> and NH<sub>3</sub> in the IR spectra.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0029_R_d1015254e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Small">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After that, the temperature gradually increased and the mass of the sample gradually decreased, which could be attributed to the production of CO during the subsequent carbonization and activation process. Following, the TG curve became stable, the production of gas led to the formation of numerous microporous structures within the carbon material, resulting in enhanced open channels for fast electron/ion movement in the carbon sheets. This facilitated efficient ion transportation and established a balance between electron transport and rapid ion diffusion</p>
            
            <p>The microscopic morphology of the carbon material could be observed by SEM (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308684-fig-0002">2</a>). SXL-5 exhibited a dense and irregular lamellar structure with more channels formed between the carbon sheets, thus inducing the effective penetration of the electrolyte, and promoting the rapid electrochemical process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0002">2a,b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0030_R_d1015254e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Carbon">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-resolution SEM image indicated that the thickness of the generated nanosheet layer is ≈500&nbsp;nm, and the nanosheets are decorated with frayed edges owing to the replacement of the edge carbon atoms by heteroatoms<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0031_R_d1015254e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0002">2c</a>). Further, the composites of SXL-5 were analyzed using scanning transmission electron microscopy with energy spectroscopy (EDS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0002">2d</a>), which clearly showed that the elements of C, O, N, and S were uniformly distributed in the sheets and as well as the successful doping of the elements N and S into the carbon matrix. The morphology and structure of S, SL, and SX-5 are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). In the absence of salt templates, S formed a surface-dense lamellar structure.SL and SX-5 formed a similar lamellar structure, which was favorable for rapid ion transport.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202308684-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e6593f95-82b8-4ba5-8fe5-5af134997693/smll202308684-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e6593f95-82b8-4ba5-8fe5-5af134997693/smll202308684-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/93d357dd-26b8-46ad-a5ba-66d3179efc8f/smll202308684-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e6593f95-82b8-4ba5-8fe5-5af134997693/smll202308684-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308684-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology structure characterization. a–c) SEM images of SXL-5. d) Corresponding EDS elemental mapping images. e–g) TEM images of SXL-5.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The carbon material was partially exfoliated into well-separated layers by a low-intensity ultrasonic water bath, and the 2D carbon nanosheet structure was analyzed by transmission electron microscopy (TEM). TEM images at large sizes further confirmed the lamellar material of SXL-5 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0002">2e</a>), and with most of the microstructures being long-range disordered, some of which were nanoscale pores, as well as short-range ordered graphitic carbon layers suggesting sufficiently abundant sodium storage sites between graphitic-like layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0002">2f</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0002">2g</a> showed that the carbon material has a distinct lattice with a layer spacing of 0.35&nbsp;nm, and these interlayer distances were larger than the graphite distance (3.4&nbsp;Å), indicating that the structure facilitates the insertion/extraction of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0032_R_d1015254e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The morphology and structure of S, SL, and SX-5 are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The three carbonaceous materials exhibited lamellar structure, the S material exhibited more graphite-ordered regions, and only the SL material displayed nanopore structure, indicating that the added potassium sulfate salt template can effectively activate and create pores in carbonaceous materials.</p>
            
            <p>The microstructural features and phase compositions of S, SL, SX-5, and SXL-5 were further analyzed using XRD and Raman spectroscopy. The XRD pattern (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3a</a>) showed that the above carbon material exhibits two peaks at 25° and 43.5°, corresponding to the plane expansion of the (002) and (100) crystal planes and the deformation and dislocation of the internal lattice of carbon, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0033_R_d1015254e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The crystal plane spacing calculated according to Bragg's formula (2dsinθ&nbsp;=&nbsp;n λ) is 0.353&nbsp;nm, which is in line with that obtained from TEM and is larger than that of graphite (0.335&nbsp;nm).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0034_R_d1015254e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Chem. A">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> S atoms preferred to be doped in the interlayers, which led to an increase in the interlayer spacing, favoring the accommodation of more Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0035_R_d1015254e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nanomicro Lett">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N/S co-doping can expand the interlayer spacing of the carbon matrix. The degree of graphitization of the two samples was evaluated by Raman spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3b</a>). The D band (1333&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) corresponds to disordered carbon, and the G (1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) band reflects the symmetry and crystallinity of the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0036_R_d1015254e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Power Sources">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The (<i>I<sub>D</sub></i>/<i>I<sub>G</sub></i>) values for S, SL, SX-5, and SXL-5 calculated from fitted Raman spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) were 2.16, 2.36, 2.34, and 2.88, demonstrating that a single salt template can bring about the limited degree of defects and disorder, but a synergistic effect of two salt templates can increase defects and contribute to the occurrence of the sodium ionization reaction. More defects facilitate the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> by carbon materials.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202308684-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/722b0a7a-303a-40f1-b49c-472e19096ef7/smll202308684-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/722b0a7a-303a-40f1-b49c-472e19096ef7/smll202308684-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fb8c9dae-e515-4a15-bb00-22ee6877decf/smll202308684-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/722b0a7a-303a-40f1-b49c-472e19096ef7/smll202308684-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308684-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern; b) Raman spectra, c) The rough XPS scanning spectra, d) The high-resolution XPS of C 1s for SXL-5, e) Schematic illustration of N, S co-doped carbon matrix. f,g) The high-resolution N 1s and S 2p XPS of SXL-5. h) Nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms and i) Pore size distribution of S, SL, SX-5, and SXL-5.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The elemental composition and bonding configurations of SXL and different salt-templated carbon materials were evaluated by XPS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3c</a>). The elemental content statistics obtained from XPS measurements are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The results showed that the addition of both salt templates increased the heteroatom content. Among them, the N and S contents of SXL-5 are higher, reaching 5.27% and 2.6%, which is beneficial to providing more structural distortion and inducing a synergistic effect to boost the sodium storage performance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3d</a> showed that the C 1s spectrum is deconvoluted into four peaks at 284.6, 286.0, 288.6, and 290.1&nbsp;eV, which are consistent with the C─C bond, C─N/C─S bond, C═O bond, and O─C═O band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0037_R_d1015254e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of C─N/C─S bonds implies the successful realization of N and S doping by the salt template combustion-activation approach. The possible states of N and S atoms in the carbon skeleton are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3e</a>. The N 1s spectrum can be fitted into three components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0011_R_d1015254e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Int. J. Energy Res.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Pyridine-N and pyrrole-N show two peaks at 398.1 and 400.1&nbsp;eV, which can promote surface-induced fast electrochemical kinetics. Another peak at 402.9&nbsp;eV is graphite- N, which can bring additional free electrons to improve the conductivity of graphene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3f</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The high-resolution S spectra clearly showed three peaks at 163.4, 164.5, and 168.5&nbsp;eV binding energies, attributed to the S2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks of the C─S─C covalent bond and the C–SO<sub>X</sub>–C peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3g</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0038_R_d1015254e1111" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Storage Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the binding energies of the C–SO<sub>X</sub>–C in SXL-5 were slightly higher than those of the characteristic peaks of S, SL, and SX-5<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0026_R_d1015254e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). It suggested that the binding energies of S─S chains were slightly longer than those of elemental S as the sulfur content increased, the S─S chain became longer and its binding energy was close to that of elemental S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0039_R_d1015254e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus the synergistic effect of N, S codoping will generate more defects/active sites and larger interlayer spacing, resulting in an improvement in electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <p>The specific surface area and porous structure of the SXL series materials were analyzed by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3h</a>). The adsorption-desorption curves conformed to type IV isotherms and H4 hysteresis return lines with microporous structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0040_R_d1015254e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The SXL-5 catalyst was measured to have a specific surface area of up to 646.32&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and abundant micropores (&lt;2&nbsp;nm) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0003">3i</a>). The microporous structure increases the contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte, providing more active sites for energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0041_R_d1015254e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Power Sources">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the activator-free S only had a specific surface area of 3.23&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> without the presence of voids(Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). It indicates that the generation of pores is favored by the synergistic activation of K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and KNO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0042_R_d1015254e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, The micropores increase the area of contact between the sample and the electrolyte, ensuring a larger specific capacitance and energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0043_R_d1015254e1179" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Carbon Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The larger surface area and microporous structure of SXL-5 ensure sufficient contact between the electrolyte and the electrode material, which is beneficial for the accommodation of more Na<sup>+</sup> and the transfer of electrons.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202308684-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202308684-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium Storage Performance and Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the effect of KNO<sub>3</sub> activator on the sodium storage performance of SIBs, the electrochemical rate performance of SXL-3, SXL-4, SXL-5, SXL-6, and SXL-7.5 were tested (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308684-fig-0004">4a</a>), and their reversible specific capacities were 202.68, 255.10, 344.44, 283.61, and 228.77&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. During the cycling process, the maximum specific capacity of SXL-5 material is 361.67&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The increase in energy storage capacity is mainly attributed to the full contact between the carbon material and the electrolyte during the cycling process, which fully activates the electrode material as well as the increase of unstable energy storage sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0044_R_d1015254e1210" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Carbon">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202308684-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eeff7223-e772-4627-949e-b31d906eace5/smll202308684-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eeff7223-e772-4627-949e-b31d906eace5/smll202308684-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/40056a61-880a-4a0f-969c-b7ad0b12a0b0/smll202308684-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eeff7223-e772-4627-949e-b31d906eace5/smll202308684-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308684-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Different amounts of potassium nitrate (SXL-x(<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3, 4, 5, 6 and 7.5)of a) Rate performance at current densities ranging. b) The 1st cycle galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles at a current density of 0.05–10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) The variation of specific capacity and initial coulombic efficiency of carbon materials with the different components of precursor. d) EIS curves of S, SL, SX-5, and SXL-5. e) Comparison of rate performance with the typical anode reported previously. f) Cyclic performance of SXL-5 at a current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The multiplicative rate performance at different currents is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). As the amount of KNO<sub>3</sub> increased, the capacity showed a trend of increasing and then decreasing. From the st cycle galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles determined the mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in SXL-5 is dominated by adsorption (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0004">4b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0045_R_d1015254e1253" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The effect of different salt additions on the porous carbon properties was compared by electrochemical rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0004">4c</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). For the S material containing only the precursor, the reversible capacity was only 115.23&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE value of only 36.89%. By bringing a small amount of microporous structure and heteroatom doping after template activation with the addition of potassium nitrate salt, the capacity was enhanced to 138.62&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, but the ICE of the material was reduced to 33.80%. By adding potassium sulfate salt with bifunctionality had a higher capacity of 303.47&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and the ICE is improved by a factor of 1.74 in comparison to S material. The SXL-5 material prepared by the synergistic action of two salt templates resulted in a carbon structure with the best sodium storage performance, with a capacity of 344.44&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the ICE was increased to 67.48%. N/S atom doping improved the wettability of electrode materials improving ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0046_R_d1015254e1274" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Storage Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The comparison of the multiplicity performance of different salt templates is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The sodium storage mechanism surface of carbon materials is open pore adsorption at 3–1&nbsp;V and defective graphite layer adsorption and graphite interlayer insertion at 1–0&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0047_R_d1015254e1282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of different salt template materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0004">4d</a>. The circular region in the high-frequency region was associated with charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and the straight line in the low-frequency region was associated with Warburg impedance (Zw), which indicated the ion diffusion rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0048_R_d1015254e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The EIS fitting results showed that the SXL-5 carbon material had the lowest <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value (35.46&nbsp;Ω). The minimum charge-transfer resistance of the SXL-5 electrode indicated that the reaction kinetics were good and showed the ability to promote electron/ion transfer at the interface of the electrode, thus accelerating its rate (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequently, the long-cycle test was used to evaluate the practical application value. As of now, the rate performance of SXL-5 was superior to other carbon materials among the SIB anodes reported in the current literature, such as BNC-600,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0037_R_d1015254e1307" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NSC-SP,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0049_R_d1015254e1312" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SC-800,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0050_R_d1015254e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Eng. J.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and SNHC<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0033_R_d1015254e1323" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0004">4e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Subsequently, the long-cycle test was used to evaluate the practical application value. The reversible capacity of SXL-5 was 143.08&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Nevertheless, after 5000 cycles, the reversible capacity was maintained at 86.12&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with excellent cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0004">4f</a>). As a result, SXL-5 had an optimal multiplicity performance and excellent stability. Suitable heteroatom doping and high specific surface area endow the material surface to generate more energy storage sites for reversible adsorption, which was favorable for sodium ion storage.</p>
            
            <p>The kinetic properties of the SXL-5 electrode were deeply investigated by Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves. The CV curves at different scanning rates are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5a</a>, and the peak position did not change with the increase in scanning rate, indicating that the material has a rapid sodiation and desodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0051_R_d1015254e1353" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy &amp; Environmental Materials">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The current (<i>i</i>) and scanning rate (<i>v</i>) obey the following power law relationship: <i>i</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;av<sup>b</sup>. When <i>b</i> value of 0.5 was the ideal diffusion-controlled process, and <i>b</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1 was the surface capacitance-controlled process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0052_R_d1015254e1372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Power Sources">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <i>b</i> values of the cathodic peak and the anodic peak of the SXL in the ether-based electrolyte are 0.87 and 0.56, indicating that the capacitive process is dominant (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5b</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202308684-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19332922-6050-405a-8296-fd262765b71a/smll202308684-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/19332922-6050-405a-8296-fd262765b71a/smll202308684-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/66e31a5b-42e6-4fd1-9f32-fd22877dbf04/smll202308684-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19332922-6050-405a-8296-fd262765b71a/smll202308684-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308684-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of SXL-5 in the ether-based electrolyte as the half-cell anode. a) CV curves of SXL-5 in different electrolytes at a scan rate of 0.2–1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Log(i)–log(v) curves. c) Capacitive contribution at the scanning rate of 1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. d) The contribution ratio of the capacitive capacity at different scan rates. e) Corresponding ex situ XRD patterns at different charge/discharge potentials. f) Ex situ Raman spectra. g) Ex situ XPS test in the first cycle; corresponding changes of h) N 1s and i) S 2p peaks at the different discharge/charge processes. j)Schematic illustration of the sodium storage process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The storage mechanism could be determined by a power law relationship as follows: <i>i</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>k</i><sub>1</sub> <i>v</i>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>k</i><sub>2</sub> <i>v</i> <sup>1/2</sup>. where <i>i</i> was the current; <i>v</i> was the sweep speed; <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> were constants and <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represented the contributions from the pseudocapacitance and diffusion processes, respectively. For SXL-5, the green shaded area represented the 88.56% capacitance contribution of the anode at 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5c</a>). A pair of reversible redox peaks (1.74/2.5&nbsp;V) appears in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5c</a>, indicating that the doped sulfur atoms can react directly with sodium in the potential window of the Na–S cell system. (2Na + x S ↔ Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub>, 3 &lt; x ≤ 8).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0039_R_d1015254e1461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The capacitive contribution ratios to the total capacity for samples at different scan rates were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5d</a>. Because of the unique structure and heteroatom doping of SXL-5, a considerable capacitance contribution was generated, which provides sufficient space for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and storage.</p>
            
            <p>In order to investigate the contribution of heteroatom doping to the sodium storage, the absence of significant peak displacement by ex situ XRD confirmed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5e</a>) that the main sodium storage mechanism of the material was not caused by carbon intercalation but by defected causing a sodium adsorption mechanism, compatible with the results of electrochemical tests.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0053_R_d1015254e1477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Carbon Energy">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As indicated by the ex-situ Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5f</a>), the <i>I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub></i> value decreased from 3.48 of the initial sample to 2.23 during discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V, which reflected the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> in the pores, defects, and edged of the carbon surface. During charging to 3&nbsp;V, the <i>I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub></i> value gradually increased to 3.00 which proved the existence of some Na<sup>+</sup> reversible reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0054_R_d1015254e1502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ex situ XPS was tested to determine the effect of N/S heteroatom doping on sodium ion storage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5g</a>). During discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, the pyridine nitrogen was sodiated, and the pyridine-N pyrrole-N, and graphite- N binding energies shifted leftward meaning that the charge was transferred from Na to the N-doped atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0055_R_d1015254e1511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Small">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During charging, the opposite process occurred (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5h</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The peaks at the ex-situ S 2p during discharge, Na<sup>+</sup> insertion appearing with NaSOx (167.2&nbsp;eV) and thiosulfate/-SO<sub>2</sub>- (169.0 and 170.0&nbsp;eV) were due to internal disproportionation reactions forming sodium sulfonate and polysulfate complexes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0056_R_d1015254e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Mater.">56</a>, <a href="#smll202308684-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0057_R_d1015254e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Energy Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The provision of an active interface through the thiosulfate intermediate ensured good stability of the material during cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5i</a>). The electrochemical storage mechanism of as-prepared carbon nanosheets can be illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0005">5j</a>. Heteroatom doping contributes to the enhancement of the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0044_R_d1015254e1540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Carbon">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sodium storage could be mainly attributed to the capacitive behavior which suggested that the surfaces, defects, doped atoms, and pores were the main storage sites during the whole discharge process, while insertion behaviors were unobvious indicating few storage sites contributions.</p>
            
            <p>Ester-based electrolytes are common electrolytes used currently, yet during use a thick solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film forms on the anode surface, resulting in low ICE and severe polarization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0058_R_d1015254e1548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ether-based electrolytes are now commonly used to improve the multiplicity morphology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0059_R_d1015254e1553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Mater. Chem. A">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the mechanism of ether and ester electrolytes leading to different electrochemical properties is still controversial at present.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0060_R_d1015254e1558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrochemical rate performance of SLX-5 anode material under Ester-based/Ether-based electrolytes was tested separately to explore the mechanism of its electrochemical performance (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308684-fig-0006">6a</a>). The ether-based electrolyte exhibited a high reversible capacity of 344.44&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (ICE&nbsp;=&nbsp;67.30%), whereas the ester-based electrolyte only exhibited a reversible capacity of 230.79&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a low ICE of 26.67%. The ICE of sodium-ion in ether electrolytes was ≈2.5 times higher than that in ester electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0006">6b</a>). An irreversible reaction of SEI formation was observed in the initial cycling in both electrolytes, as evidenced by the sharp redox peak ≈0.3&nbsp;V of the initial CV curve in the ester-based electrolyte<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0061_R_d1015254e1580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Adv. Mater.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0006">6c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Nonetheless, no sharp redox peaks were found in the ether-based electrolyte, suggesting the formation of a thinner SEI. The storage mechanism of the ester-based electrolyte was calculated by the power law relationship between current and sweep rate. The capacitance contributions in the ester-based electrolyte ester-based electrolytes 54.13%, 59.09%, 64.65%, 68.86%, and 72.08% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308684-fig-0006">6d</a>) were lower than ether-based electrolytes. It was related to the weak coordination of solvent molecules with Na<sup>+</sup> in the ether-based electrolyte, leading to the adsorption of the carbon component, which favored the smooth reaction of carbon materials with sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308684-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308684-bib-0032_R_d1015254e1597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202308684-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97c60de5-d821-456b-8f0f-9675d2e73134/smll202308684-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/97c60de5-d821-456b-8f0f-9675d2e73134/smll202308684-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05cf2ef2-cf8d-4b4f-a0a6-04be8d98ba05/smll202308684-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97c60de5-d821-456b-8f0f-9675d2e73134/smll202308684-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308684-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308684" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of SXL as the half-cell anode in the ether- or ester-based electrolyte. a) Rate performance. b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curve for the first cycles at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) CV curves for the initial cycles in different electrolytes at a scan rate of 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. d). The contribution ratio of the capacitive capacity at different scan rates. f) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were calculated from the GITT potential profiles. g) EIS analysis. h) Cycling performance at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>During the discharge process, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) was calculated using the constant-current intermittent titration technique (GITT) to further analyze the differences in the kinetic behaviors of SXL-5 in different electrolytes during the discharge process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value could be calculated according to Fick's second law using the following simplified equation:<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3998a130-a67b-4412-83ff-0e80458bb47a/smll202308684-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202308684:smll202308684-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202308684-math-0005.png">
